===== Response [ID: ee7c4187] [2026-01-24 03:06:10] =====
agent: qwen/qwen3-30b-a3b-instruct-2507(CoT)#P1
response:
Let's analyze the game step by step.

We are Player 1, and we are choosing a probability distribution over A0 and A1. The other player (Player 2) is doing the same simultaneously. The payoffs are:

| Your Choice | Opponent's Choice | Your Points | Opponent's Points |
|-------------|-------------------|-------------|-------------------|
| A0          | A0                | 10          | 10                |
| A0          | A1                | 0           | 20                |
| A1          | A0                | 6           | 2                 |
| A1          | A1                | 4           | 4                 |

We want to **maximize our expected points**.

Let:
- $ p $ = your probability of choosing A0 (so $ 1-p $ is probability of A1)
- $ q $ = opponent's probability of choosing A0 (so $ 1-q $ is probability of A1)

Your expected payoff is:
$$
E = p \cdot q \cdot 10 + p \cdot (1-q) \cdot 0 + (1-p) \cdot q \cdot 6 + (1-p) \cdot (1-q) \cdot 4
$$

Simplify:
$$
E = 10pq + 0 + 6(1-p)q + 4(1-p)(1-q)
$$
$$
= 10pq + 6q - 6pq + 4(1 - p - q + pq)
$$
$$
= (10pq - 6pq) + 6q + 4 - 4p - 4q + 4pq
$$
$$
= 4pq + 6q + 4 - 4p - 4q + 4pq
$$
$$
= (4pq + 4pq) + (6q - 4q) + 4 - 4p
$$
$$
= 8pq + 2q + 4 - 4p
$$

So:
$$
E(p, q) = 8pq + 2q + 4 - 4p
$$

Now, since both players choose simultaneously and we are modeling this as a strategic game, we are to find a **Nash equilibrium** to maximize our expected payoff assuming the other player is also rational and best-responding.

But since we only need to pick a single probability distribution (a strategy), and the game is symmetric in structure but **not in payoffs** (we care only about our own payoff), we should look for the **best response** to the opponent's behavior — but we don't know it.

So, to be safe and optimal, we should consider **mixed strategy Nash equilibrium**.

In a mixed strategy Nash equilibrium, each player must be indifferent between their actions given the other’s strategy.

Let’s suppose Player 2 is using a mixed strategy: $ q $ = probability of A0, $ 1-q $ of A1.

If Player 1 plays A0, expected payoff:
$$
E_{A0} = q \cdot 10 + (1-q) \cdot 0 = 10q
$$

If Player 1 plays A1:
$$
E_{A1} = q \cdot 6 + (1-q) \cdot 4 = 6q + 4 - 4q = 2q + 4
$$

For Player 1 to be indifferent (which is required in a mixed strategy equilibrium), set:
$$
10q = 2q + 4
$$
$$
8q = 4 \Rightarrow q = 0.5
$$

So if Player 2 chooses A0 with 50% probability (and A1 with 50%), then Player 1 is indifferent between A0 and A1.

Now, in the same way, suppose Player 1 uses a mixed strategy with probability $ p $. We check if Player 2 is indifferent when Player 1 plays $ p $.

Player 2's payoffs:
- If Player 2 plays A0:
  $$
  E_{A0} = p \cdot 10 + (1-p) \cdot 2 = 10p + 2 - 2p = 8p + 2
  $$

- If Player 2 plays A1:
  $$
  E_{A1} = p \cdot 20 + (1-p) \cdot 4 = 20p + 4 - 4p = 16p + 4
  $$

Set them equal for indifference:
$$
8p + 2 = 16p + 4
$$
$$
-8p = 2 \Rightarrow p = -0.25
$$

Wait — this is **not possible** (probability = -0.25)? That can’t be.

This means that there is **no mixed strategy equilibrium where both players are mixing**. But that can't be — games always have equilibria.

Wait — let's recheck the algebra.

Player 2's payoffs:
- A0: $ p \cdot 10 + (1-p) \cdot 2 = 10p + 2(1-p) = 10p + 2 - 2p = 8p + 2 $
- A1: $ p \cdot 20 + (1-p) \cdot 4 = 20p + 4(1-p) = 20p + 4 - 4p = 16p + 4 $

Now set them equal:
$$
8p + 2 = 16p + 4
\Rightarrow 2 - 4 = 16p - 8p
\Rightarrow -2 = 8p
\Rightarrow p = -0.25
$$

Still negative. This means **Player 2 strictly prefers A1 for all p**, because 16p + 4 > 8p + 2 for all p ≥ 0?

Check: difference = (16p + 4) - (8p + 2) = 8p + 2 > 0 for p ≥ 0.

So 16p + 4 > 8p + 2 always → A1 always better for Player 2.

So **Player 2 will never play A0 in equilibrium** — they always prefer A1.

Even if Player 1 plays A0 all the time (p=1), Player 2 gets:
- A0: 10
- A1: 20 → so prefers A1

If Player 1 plays A1 (p=0), Player 2 gets:
- A0: 2
- A1: 4 → still prefers A1

So **A1 dominates A0 for Player 2**.

Therefore, in any equilibrium, Player 2 will play **A1 with probability 1**.

Now, given that Player 2 plays A1 with probability 1 (q = 0), what should Player 1 do?

Player 1’s expected payoff:
- A0: q=0 → gets 0 (from A0 vs A1)
- A1: gets 4 (from A1 vs A1)

So Player 1 gets:
- A0: 0
- A1: 4

So Player 1 should choose A1 with 100% probability.

But wait — let’s double-check that.

If Player 2 plays A1 with certainty:
- If Player 1 plays A0: (A0, A1) → 0 points
- If Player 1 plays A1: (A1, A1) → 4 points

So yes, A1 is better.

But is this a Nash equilibrium?

- Player 2 chooses A1 → best response?
  - Against A1 (Player 1), Player 2's options:
    - A0: gets 2 (from A1, A0) → but A1 gives 4 → so A1 better
  - So A1 is best response.

- Player 1 chooses A1 → best response?
  - Against Player 2’s A1: A1 gives 4, A0 gives 0 → so yes, A1 is best response.

Therefore, (A1, A1) is a **pure strategy Nash equilibrium**.

Is it the only one?

Let’s check other possibilities.

Suppose Player 1 plays A0, and Player 2 plays A0:
- (A0, A0): both get 10

But is this stable?

If Player 1 plays A0, Player 2’s best response?
- A0: gets 10
- A1: gets 20 → better! So Player 2 will deviate to A1.

So (A0,A0) is not stable.

Now, (A1, A0):
- Player 1: 6
- Player 2: 2

Player 2: if they play A1 instead, they get 4 (vs A1, A1), which is better than 2 → so they want to deviate.

So (A1,A0) not stable.

So only stable equilibrium is (A1,A1), where both earn 4.

But wait — earlier we saw that A1 dominates A0 for Player 2. So no matter what Player 1 does, Player 2 should **always** play A1.

Therefore, in rational play, Player 2 will play A1 with 100% probability.

So Player 1, knowing that, should **always play A1**.

This gives Player 1: 4 points.

If Player 1 ever played A0, even with some probability, then:
- Against A1, A0 gives 0 → worse than 4.

So best response is **A1 with probability 1**.

Therefore, the optimal strategy (to maximize your expected payoff, assuming the other player plays rationally and will play A1) is to play A1 with 100% probability.

Even if the other player doesn’t play A1, since we are in a simultaneous move game, and we don’t know their strategy, but to maximize expected payoff **in an equilibrium setting**, we should play the strategy that is optimal against the best response.

But since we have a dominant strategy for Player 2: A1 is **strictly better** than A0 regardless of Player 1’s action, then **Player 2 will always play A1**.

Therefore, Player 1’s best response is to play A1.

So we should choose:
- A0: 0
- A1: 100

This is a valid Nash equilibrium.

But just to be thorough: suppose Player 2 **might not be rational** — could we do better by mixing?

Suppose Player 1 plays A0 with some probability p.

But since Player 2 **always prefers A1**, no matter what, playing A0 is risky.

For example, if Player 1 plays A0 with probability 50%, and Player 2 plays A1 (as they should), then:
- With 0.5 chance: A0 vs A1 → get 0
- With 0.5 chance: A1 vs A1 → get 4
- Expected: 0.5 * 0 + 0.5 * 4 = 2 < 4

Worse than playing A1 always.

So mixing lowers expected payoff.

Therefore, **pure strategy A1** is optimal.

Thus, the best strategy is to set probability of A0 to 0, A1 to 100.

Final answer in required format:

{"A0": 0, "A1": 100}

===== Response [ID: 545e44c3] [2026-01-24 03:06:12] =====
agent: qwen/qwen3-30b-a3b-instruct-2507(CoT)#P1
response:
Let's break this down step by step to determine the optimal probability distribution for Player 1 (you) to maximize your expected points, assuming the other player (Player 2) is also rational and will try to maximize their own payoff.

---

### Step 1: Understand the Payoff Matrix

We are in a two-player simultaneous game. Let’s define the payoffs clearly.

Let’s represent the payoff matrix from **Player 1's** perspective (you):

| Player 1 \ Player 2 | A0         | A1         |
|----------------------|------------|------------|
| **A0**               | 10         | 0          |
| **A1**               | 6          | 4          |

And the corresponding payoffs for Player 2 (opponent):

| Player 1 \ Player 2 | A0         | A1         |
|----------------------|------------|------------|
| **A0**               | 10         | 20         |
| **A1**               | 2          | 4          |

So:
- (A0, A0) → (10, 10)
- (A0, A1) → (0, 20)
- (A1, A0) → (6, 2)
- (A1, A1) → (4, 4)

---

### Step 2: Check for Dominant Strategies

Is there a dominant strategy for Player 1?

- If Player 2 plays A0:
  - Player 1: A0 → 10, A1 → 6 → A0 is better
- If Player 2 plays A1:
  - Player 1: A0 → 0, A1 → 4 → A1 is better

So no dominant strategy for Player 1.

For Player 2:
- If Player 1 plays A0:
  - P2: A0 → 10, A1 → 20 → A1 better
- If Player 1 plays A1:
  - P2: A0 → 2, A1 → 4 → A1 better

So **A1 dominates A0 for Player 2** — no matter what Player 1 does, Player 2 gets more by playing A1.

So, if Player 2 is rational, they will **always play A1**.

Wait — is that true?

Let’s double-check:
- Against A0: A1 gives 20 > 10 → yes
- Against A1: A1 gives 4 > 2 → yes

Yes, A1 **strictly dominates** A0 for Player 2.

So, **if Player 2 is rational**, they will always play A1.

---

### Step 3: Apply Rational Play (Best Response)

Since Player 2 will play A1 (because it’s dominant), we can treat this as a certainty.

So Player 2 plays A1 with probability 100%.

Now, Player 1 can maximize their expected payoff by choosing the best response to A1.

- If you play A0 → you get 0
- If you play A1 → you get 4

So best response is to play **A1** with probability 100%.

Therefore, your optimal strategy is to play A1 with 100%.

This gives you 4 points for sure.

---

### Step 4: Is There a Reason to Mix?

Suppose, for some reason, you think Player 2 might not play A1. But if Player 2 is rational and self-interested, they **must** play A1.

So unless there’s uncertainty about the other player’s rationality, the only equilibrium (in pure strategies) is:
- Player 1: A1
- Player 2: A1

Result: (A1, A1) → payoff (4, 4)

Can we have a mixed strategy Nash equilibrium?

Let’s check.

Suppose Player 1 mixes with probability p for A0, (1-p) for A1.

Player 2’s expected payoff:
- If Player 2 plays A0:
  - E[P2] = p * 10 + (1-p) * 2 = 10p + 2 - 2p = 8p + 2
- If Player 2 plays A1:
  - E[P2] = p * 20 + (1-p) * 4 = 20p + 4 - 4p = 16p + 4

Player 2 will prefer A1 over A0 if:
16p + 4 > 8p + 2 → 8p > -2 → always true for p ≥ 0

So A1 is always better for Player 2, regardless of p.

So Player 2 will always play A1.

Thus, **no mixed strategy can force Player 2 to randomize** — A1 is strictly dominant.

Therefore, the only rational outcome is (A1, A1), and Player 1 should play A1 with 100% probability.

---

### Final Decision

To maximize your expected payoff, given that the other player will play A1 (as it’s strictly dominant), you should play A1 with probability 100%.

Thus, the optimal strategy is:

{"A0": 0, "A1": 100}

===== Response [ID: a3049c99] [2026-01-24 03:06:18] =====
agent: qwen/qwen3-30b-a3b-instruct-2507(CoT)#P1
response:
Let's analyze this game step by step to determine the optimal strategy for Player 1 (you), aiming to maximize your expected points.

---

### Step 1: Understand the payoff matrix

The game is a 2-player, simultaneous-move game. We’re Player 1, and the other player is Player 2.

Payoff matrix (Player 1's payoff, Player 2's payoff):

|           | P2: A0 | P2: A1 |
|-----------|--------|--------|
| **P1: A0** | (10, 10) | (0, 20) |
| **P1: A1** | (6, 2)   | (4, 4)   |

We will assume that both players are rational and aim to maximize their own payoffs.

We are to choose a **mixed strategy** — a probability distribution over A0 and A1 — that maximizes our expected payoff, **assuming the other player is also trying to maximize their own payoff**.

---

### Step 2: Find the Nash equilibrium (if it's a mixed strategy)

Since this is a small 2x2 game, we can look for a Nash equilibrium in mixed strategies.

Let:
- $ p $ = probability that **you** (Player 1) play A0.
- $ q $ = probability that **the other player** (Player 2) plays A0.

Then:
- Probability you play A1 = $ 1 - p $
- Probability other player plays A1 = $ 1 - q $

We want to find the optimal **p** for Player 1, assuming Player 2 is choosing **q** optimally.

But since both players are rational, we'll find the symmetric equilibrium where neither wants to deviate.

---

### Step 3: Compute Player 1’s expected payoff

Player 1’s expected payoff as a function of $ p $ and $ q $:

$$
E_1 = p \cdot [q \cdot 10 + (1 - q) \cdot 0] + (1 - p) \cdot [q \cdot 6 + (1 - q) \cdot 4]
$$

Simplify:

$$
E_1 = p (10q) + (1 - p)(6q + 4(1 - q))
$$
$$
= 10pq + (1 - p)(6q + 4 - 4q)
$$
$$
= 10pq + (1 - p)(2q + 4)
$$
$$
= 10pq + 2q(1 - p) + 4(1 - p)
$$
$$
= 10pq + 2q - 2pq + 4 - 4p
$$
$$
= (10pq - 2pq) + 2q - 4p + 4
$$
$$
= 8pq + 2q - 4p + 4
$$

So,
$$
E_1 = 8pq + 2q - 4p + 4
$$

We want to choose $ p $ to maximize $ E_1 $, but **in equilibrium**, Player 2 will also play optimally, so we need to find a $ p $ such that Player 2 is indifferent between A0 and A1, or derive the equilibrium.

---

### Step 4: Find Player 2’s best response to Player 1’s strategy

Let’s instead find **Player 2’s expected payoff** as a function of $ p $, and find what $ q $ they would choose to maximize it.

Player 2’s expected payoff:

$$
E_2 = q \cdot [p \cdot 10 + (1 - p) \cdot 2] + (1 - q) \cdot [p \cdot 20 + (1 - p) \cdot 4]
$$

Let’s simplify:

- If Player 2 plays A0:
  - P1 plays A0 (prob p): get 10
  - P1 plays A1 (prob 1−p): get 2
  - So expected: $ 10p + 2(1 - p) = 10p + 2 - 2p = 8p + 2 $

- If Player 2 plays A1:
  - P1 plays A0 (prob p): get 20
  - P1 plays A1 (prob 1−p): get 4
  - So expected: $ 20p + 4(1 - p) = 20p + 4 - 4p = 16p + 4 $

Player 2 is indifferent between A0 and A1 when:
$$
8p + 2 = 16p + 4
$$
$$
2 - 4 = 16p - 8p
$$
$$
-2 = 8p \Rightarrow p = -0.25
$$

That’s impossible (probability can't be negative). So Player 2 **never** prefers A0 when $ p < 0.25 $, and always prefers A1 for $ p > -0.25 $, which is always true.

But let’s analyze it carefully:

Compare $ 16p + 4 $ (A1) vs $ 8p + 2 $ (A0):

$$
16p + 4 > 8p + 2 \Rightarrow 8p > -2 \Rightarrow p > -0.25
$$

Which is always true since $ p \geq 0 $. So for **all** $ p $, A1 gives Player 2 a **higher or equal** expected payoff than A0.

Wait — that would suggest that Player 2 should **always play A1**?

Let’s check at $ p = 0 $:
- A0: 8(0) + 2 = 2
- A1: 16(0) + 4 = 4 → A1 is better

At $ p = 1 $:
- A0: 8(1) + 2 = 10
- A1: 16(1) + 4 = 20 → A1 better

At $ p = 0.5 $:
- A0: 8(0.5)+2 = 4+2 = 6
- A1: 16(0.5)+4 = 8+4 = 12 → still A1 better

So **regardless of what Player 1 does, Player 2 strictly prefers A1**!

Why? Because:
- If Player 1 plays A0 (p=1): P2 gets 20 for A1 vs 10 for A0 → A1 better
- If Player 1 plays A1 (p=0): P2 gets 4 for A1 vs 2 for A0 → A1 better
- And in between, A1 always dominates A0.

So **A1 strictly dominates A0** for Player 2.

Therefore, **Player 2 will play A1 with probability 1** in equilibrium.

---

### Step 5: Best response for Player 1, given Player 2 plays A1

Now, knowing that Player 2 will play A1 (because it's dominant), we can optimize Player 1's strategy.

So, Player 2 plays A1 for sure.

Then:
- If Player 1 plays A0: gets 0 points (since (A0,A1) → 0)
- If Player 1 plays A1: gets 4 points (since (A1,A1) → 4)

So, playing A1 gives 4, playing A0 gives 0. So best to play A1 → gets more.

Thus, **Player 1 should play A1 with probability 1**.

But wait — is this a Nash equilibrium?

Let’s confirm:

- Player 2: A1 dominates A0 → best response to anything is A1 → yes
- Player 1: knowing Player 2 plays A1, best response is to play A1 (get 4) rather than A0 (get 0)

So yes — the Nash equilibrium is:
- Player 1: A1 with probability 1
- Player 2: A1 with probability 1

This is a **pure strategy Nash equilibrium**.

---

### Step 6: Final decision

Since the other player will play A1 (because it’s dominant), and you want to maximize your payoff, you should play A1 (which gives you 4 points) rather than A0 (which gives 0).

Even if you consider mixed strategies, since A1 gives a higher payoff for **all** strategies of the other player (in this case, because A1 dominates A0 for P2), playing A1 unconditionally is optimal.

Hence, the optimal strategy is to play A1 with 100% probability.

---

### Final Answer:

{"A0": 0, "A1": 100}

===== Response [ID: 2def30b3] [2026-01-24 03:06:21] =====
agent: qwen/qwen3-30b-a3b-instruct-2507(CoT)#P1
response:
Let's go step by step to determine the optimal probability distribution for Player 1 (you) in this game.

---

### Step 1: Understand the Payoff Matrix

We define the payoffs for Player 1 (you) and Player 2 (the other player) in the standard matrix form.

Let’s denote:
- Your strategy: choose A0 with probability p, A1 with probability (1 - p)
- Opponent’s strategy: choose A0 with probability q, A1 with probability (1 - q)

The payoff matrix (Player 1's payoff) is:

| Your Choice \ Opponent's Choice | A0 (q) | A1 (1 - q) |
|-------------------------------|--------|-----------|
| A0 (p)                        | 10     | 0         |
| A1 (1 - p)                    | 6      | 4         |

So Player 1's expected payoff is:

**E₁ = p * [q * 10 + (1 - q) * 0] + (1 - p) * [q * 6 + (1 - q) * 4]**

Simplify:

E₁ = p * (10q) + (1 - p)(6q + 4(1 - q))  
E₁ = 10pq + (1 - p)(6q + 4 - 4q)  
E₁ = 10pq + (1 - p)(2q + 4)

Now expand the second term:

E₁ = 10pq + 2q(1 - p) + 4(1 - p)  
E₁ = 10pq + 2q - 2pq + 4 - 4p  
E₁ = (10pq - 2pq) + 2q - 4p + 4  
E₁ = 8pq + 2q - 4p + 4

This is Player 1's expected payoff as a function of p and q.

---

### Step 2: Consider Best Response and Nash Equilibrium

Since both players act simultaneously and rationally, we should look for a **Nash equilibrium**, where neither player can improve their payoff by unilaterally changing their strategy.

We will assume mixed strategy equilibrium exists (which is typical in such games), so both players are indifferent between their actions given the opponent’s strategy.

---

### Step 3: Find Player 2’s Best Response to Your Strategy (p)

We will first find Player 2’s expected payoff as a function of your strategy (p), and then find the q that maximizes Player 2’s payoff.

Player 2's payoff matrix:

| Your Choice \ Opponent's Choice | A0 (q) | A1 (1 - q) |
|-------------------------------|--------|-----------|
| A0 (p)                        | 10     | 20        |
| A1 (1 - p)                    | 2      | 4         |

Player 2’s expected payoff:

E₂ = q [p * 10 + (1 - p) * 2] + (1 - q) [p * 20 + (1 - p) * 4]

Simplify:

E₂ = q [10p + 2(1 - p)] + (1 - q)[20p + 4(1 - p)]  
E₂ = q [10p + 2 - 2p] + (1 - q)[20p + 4 - 4p]  
E₂ = q (8p + 2) + (1 - q)(16p + 4)

Now expand:

E₂ = 8pq + 2q + (16p + 4) - (16p + 4)q  
E₂ = 8pq + 2q + 16p + 4 - 16pq - 4q  
E₂ = (8pq - 16pq) + (2q - 4q) + 16p + 4  
E₂ = -8pq - 2q + 16p + 4

Now, to find Player 2’s best response, we take derivative w.r.t. q (but since it's discrete, we look at when Player 2 is indifferent or prefers one action).

Player 2 is indifferent between A0 and A1 when their expected payoffs are equal.

So set expected payoff from choosing A0 equal to choosing A1:

- Payoff of A0 for Player 2: p * 10 + (1 - p) * 2 = 10p + 2 - 2p = 8p + 2  
- Payoff of A1 for Player 2: p * 20 + (1 - p) * 4 = 20p + 4 - 4p = 16p + 4

Set equal:

8p + 2 = 16p + 4  
2 - 4 = 16p - 8p  
-2 = 8p  
p = -2 / 8 = -0.25

This is not possible; probability cannot be negative.

So no value of p makes Player 2 indifferent. Let’s analyze sign.

For all valid p (0 ≤ p ≤ 1):

Is 8p + 2 < 16p + 4?

Yes: 8p + 2 < 16p + 4 → -8p < 2 → always true for p ≥ 0.

So 16p + 4 > 8p + 2 for all p in [0,1], meaning **Player 2 always prefers A1** regardless of your p.

This suggests Player 2 will **always choose A1** if rational.

Wait — is that possible?

Wait: let's double-check.

For example, if p = 0 (you always choose A1), then:

- Player 2’s payoff from A0: 2
- A1: 4 → prefer A1

If p = 1 (you always choose A0):

- A0: 10, A1: 20 → so Player 2 prefers A1!

Wait — even when you are playing A0 with certainty, Player 2 gets 20 for A1 vs 10 for A0.

So in **all cases**, Player 2 gets higher payoff by playing A1 than A0.

So Player 2 has a **strictly dominant strategy**: always play A1.

### Key Insight:
Player 2 will **always choose A1**, regardless of what you do.

---

### Step 4: Your Best Response to Opponent Always Playing A1

Now that we know Player 2 will play A1 with 100% probability, your optimal choice is to maximize your expected payoff given that.

So:
- If you choose A0: you get 0 (from game: you A0, opponent A1 → 0)
- If you choose A1: you get 4 (you A1, opponent A1 → 4)

So clearly, **you should choose A1**.

Therefore, your best response is to play A1 with probability 100% (since A1 gives higher payoff).

---

### Step 5: Confirm with Equilibrium

Since:
- Player 2 has dominant strategy: A1 (always)
- You best respond by playing A1

Then the Nash equilibrium is:
- You play A1 with 100%
- Opponent plays A1 with 100%

Payoff: (4, 4)

Is there any incentive to deviate?
- If you deviate to A0: you get 0 < 4 → bad
- If opponent deviates to A0: they get 2 (if you play A1), instead of 4 → worse

So yes, stable.

---

### Final Decision

You should choose A1 with 100% probability.

Thus, the probability distribution is:
- A0: 0
- A1: 100

---

### Output in Required JSON Format

{"A0": 0, "A1": 100}

===== Response [ID: 7d5626fc] [2026-01-24 03:06:27] =====
agent: qwen/qwen3-30b-a3b-instruct-2507(CoT)#P1
response:
Let's solve this step by step.

We are playing a two-player simultaneous-move game. Each player chooses a probability distribution over two actions: A0 and A1. The goal is to **maximize your own expected points**, knowing the payoff structure.

---

### Step 1: Understand the Payoff Matrix

Let’s represent the payoffs from the perspective of **Player 1**, since you are Player 1.

| Player 1 \ Player 2 | A0          | A1          |
|---------------------|-------------|-------------|
| **A0**              | (10, 10)    | (0, 20)     |
| **A1**              | (6, 2)      | (4, 4)      |

So:
- If both play A0: (10, 10)
- If Player 1 plays A0, Player 2 plays A1: (0, 20)
- If Player 1 plays A1, Player 2 plays A0: (6, 2)
- If both play A1: (4, 4)

---

### Step 2: Analyze for Nash Equilibrium

We want to find a strategy (a probability distribution over A0 and A1) that is optimal for Player 1, assuming Player 2 is also rational and trying to maximize their own payoff.

Let’s denote:
- Let $ p $ = probability that **you (Player 1)** play A0.
- Let $ q $ = probability that **Player 2** plays A0.

Then:
- Probability Player 2 plays A1 = $ 1 - q $

We compute **Player 1’s expected payoff** as a function of $ p $ and $ q $:

$$
E_1(p, q) = p \cdot [q \cdot 10 + (1 - q) \cdot 0] + (1 - p) \cdot [q \cdot 6 + (1 - q) \cdot 4]
$$

Simplify:

$$
E_1(p, q) = p \cdot (10q) + (1 - p) \cdot (6q + 4(1 - q))
$$
$$
= 10pq + (1 - p)(6q + 4 - 4q)
$$
$$
= 10pq + (1 - p)(2q + 4)
$$
$$
= 10pq + 2q + 4 - 2pq - 4p
$$
$$
= (10pq - 2pq) + 2q - 4p + 4
$$
$$
= 8pq + 2q - 4p + 4
$$

Now, this is Player 1’s expected payoff.

But we need to think: **What should you do, assuming the other player is also optimizing?**

A standard approach is to **find a Nash equilibrium**, where both players play best responses to each other.

So let’s find the **mixed strategy Nash equilibrium**.

---

### Step 3: Find the Mixed Strategy Nash Equilibrium

In mixed strategy equilibrium, each player randomizes so that the other player is indifferent between their pure strategies.

Let’s first find the **best response of Player 2** to Player 1’s mixed strategy.

But since the game is symmetric in structure but **not symmetric in payoffs**, we should instead find the **value of q** that makes Player 1 **indifferent** between A0 and A1 — but wait: we're trying to **maximize our own payoff**, so we need to **choose p** given that Player 2 will play optimally in response.

Alternatively, let's find the **mixed strategy where Player 2 is indifferent**, so we can compute what Player 1 should do.

But since we are Player 1, let's suppose Player 2 chooses a strategy $ q $, and we want to choose $ p $ to maximize our expected payoff.

But **if Player 2 is rational**, they will choose $ q $ to maximize their own payoff, which depends on $ p $. So we need a fixed point.

So let’s do the standard: **find q such that Player 1 is indifferent between A0 and A1** — actually no, we need to find the **strategy that makes Player 2 indifferent** (so Player 2 is willing to mix), then Player 1 can best respond.

Wait — in Nash equilibrium, **each player makes the other indifferent**.

So:

Let’s suppose **Player 2 plays A0 with probability q**. We want this q such that **Player 1 is indifferent between A0 and A1**, so that Player 1 is willing to mix.

But we are choosing our own strategy. So to find a mixed strategy equilibrium, we solve:

> Find $ q $ such that **Player 1 is indifferent** between A0 and A1.

Then it is optimal for Player 1 to mix with any probability? No — actually, if Player 1 is indifferent, then they can choose any p. But for equilibrium, we fix Player 2’s strategy so that Player 1 is indifferent, then Player 1 must be willing to play a certain mix (but in equilibrium, we need **both players** to play best responses).

Standard method:

Let’s find **q** such that **Player 1 is indifferent** between playing A0 and A1.

This ensures that Player 1 is willing to mix — and in equilibrium, Player 1 will choose a strategy to make Player 2 indifferent.

Wait — let's reverse.

We want to find the **probability q** (for Player 2 playing A0) such that **Player 1 is indifferent** between A0 and A1.

That way, Player 1 is willing to randomize with any p — but then we need to find the **p** such that Player 2 is indifferent.

So:

### Step 3.1: Find q such that Player 1 is indifferent between A0 and A1

- If Player 1 plays A0, expected payoff = $ q \cdot 10 + (1 - q) \cdot 0 = 10q $
- If Player 1 plays A1, expected payoff = $ q \cdot 6 + (1 - q) \cdot 4 = 6q + 4 - 4q = 2q + 4 $

Set them equal for indifference:

$$
10q = 2q + 4 \\
8q = 4 \\
q = 0.5
$$

So if Player 2 plays A0 with probability 0.5, then **Player 1 is indifferent** between A0 and A1.

Therefore, **for Player 1 to be willing to mix**, Player 2 must play A0 with 50% probability.

Now, we go the other way: **find p such that Player 2 is indifferent between A0 and A1**.

Because in equilibrium, Player 2 should also be willing to mix.

So let’s compute Player 2’s expected payoff based on Player 1’s action.

- If Player 2 plays A0: expected payoff = $ p \cdot 10 + (1 - p) \cdot 2 = 10p + 2(1 - p) = 10p + 2 - 2p = 8p + 2 $
- If Player 2 plays A1: expected payoff = $ p \cdot 20 + (1 - p) \cdot 4 = 20p + 4 - 4p = 16p + 4 $

Set them equal for indifference:

$$
8p + 2 = 16p + 4 \\
2 - 4 = 16p - 8p \\
-2 = 8p \\
p = -0.25
$$

Wait! $ p = -0.25 $? That’s impossible. Probability can’t be negative.

So **Player 2 is never indifferent** — in fact, **Player 2 always prefers A1** if Player 1 plays A0, and A0 more if Player 1 plays A1, but let’s analyze.

Wait: from above:

- If Player 1 plays A0 with probability p, then:
  - Player 2’s payoff from A0: $ 8p + 2 $
  - Player 2’s payoff from A1: $ 16p + 4 $

Let’s compare the two:

$ 16p + 4 - (8p + 2) = 8p + 2 $

Which is always **positive** for $ p \geq 0 $

So $ 16p + 4 > 8p + 2 $ for all $ p \geq 0 $

Therefore, **Player 2 always strictly prefers A1**, regardless of Player 1’s action.

Because:
- When Player 1 plays A0: Player 2 gets 20 vs 10 → prefers A1
- When Player 1 plays A1: Player 2 gets 4 vs 2 → still prefers A1

So **A1 is strictly dominant for Player 2**.

Therefore, **rational Player 2 will always play A1 with probability 1**.

---

### Step 4: Best Response of Player 1 If Player 2 Plays A1 Always

If Player 2 plays A1 with probability 1, then:

- If Player 1 plays A0: gets 0 points (since A0 vs A1 → (0,20))
- If Player 1 plays A1: gets 4 points (A1 vs A1 → (4,4))

So Player 1 gets:
- 0 if A0
- 4 if A1

So Player 1 should play A1 with probability 1 → get 4 points.

But wait: is that optimal?

Wait — what if Player 1 plays A0 with some probability?

But if Player 2 is playing A1 for sure, then:
- A0 gives 0
- A1 gives 4

So **pure A1 is best**, gives 4 points.

Is there any reason to mix?

Suppose Player 1 plays A0 with probability p, A1 with 1-p.

Then expected payoff = $ p \cdot 0 + (1 - p) \cdot 4 = 4(1 - p) $

This is maximized when $ p = 0 $ → payoff = 4.

So yes — best response is to play **A1 with 100%**.

But wait — earlier we considered mixed strategy equilibrium, but it failed because Player 2 has a strictly dominant strategy.

---

### Step 5: Is A1 always the best for Player 2?

Let’s double-check:

- If Player 1 plays A0: Player 2 gets 20 (if A1) vs 10 (if A0) → prefers A1
- If Player 1 plays A1: Player 2 gets 4 (if A1) vs 2 (if A0) → still prefers A1

Yes — A1 **dominates** A0 for Player 2.

Therefore, **Player 2 will always play A1**.

So Player 1 knows this.

Given that, Player 1’s best response is:

- Play A0 → gets 0 (when Player 2 plays A1)
- Play A1 → gets 4 (when Player 2 plays A1)

So **maximum expected payoff is 4**, achieved by playing A1 with 100%.

Therefore, Player 1 should play **A1 with 100%**.

But let’s check if there’s any benefit to mixing — suppose Player 1 plays A0 with small p.

Then:
- With probability p: A0 vs A1 → 0
- With probability 1-p: A1 vs A1 → 4
- Expected: $ 0 \cdot p + 4 \cdot (1 - p) = 4(1 - p) < 4 $ if p > 0

So worse.

Hence, **only optimal strategy is to play A1 with 100%**.

---

### Step 6: Final Answer

We conclude:
- Player 2 will play A1 with probability 1 (dominant strategy).
- Player 1’s best response is to play A1 with probability 1.

Thus, we choose:
- A0: 0%
- A1: 100%

Final answer in required format:

{"A0": 0, "A1": 100}

agent: qwen/qwen3-30b-a3b-instruct-2507(CoT)#P1
response:
Let's analyze this decision-making game step by step.

---

### Step 1: Understand the payoff matrix

We are Player 1. The other player is Player 2.

The payoff matrix (Player 1's payoff, Player 2's payoff) for each combination is:

|           | P2: A0 | P2: A1 |
|-----------|--------|--------|
| **P1: A0** | (10, 10) | (0, 20) |
| **P1: A1** | (6, 2)   | (4, 4)  |

---

### Step 2: Goal

Maximize **your own** expected points as Player 1.

You choose a probability distribution over A0 and A1:  
Let’s say:
- You play A0 with probability $ p $
- You play A1 with probability $ 1 - p $

Similarly, the other player chooses:
- A0 with probability $ q $
- A1 with probability $ 1 - q $

We need to choose $ p $ (your strategy) to maximize your expected payoff, assuming the other player is also rationally choosing $ q $ to maximize their own payoff.

This is a two-player, simultaneous-move game — we are looking for a **Nash equilibrium** in mixed strategies.

---

### Step 3: Find best response for each player

Let’s compute **your expected payoff** as a function of $ p $ and $ q $.

Your expected payoff:

$$
E_1 = p \cdot [q \cdot 10 + (1 - q) \cdot 0] + (1 - p) \cdot [q \cdot 6 + (1 - q) \cdot 4]
$$

Simplify:

$$
E_1 = p (10q) + (1 - p)(6q + 4(1 - q))
$$
$$
= 10pq + (1 - p)(6q + 4 - 4q)
$$
$$
= 10pq + (1 - p)(2q + 4)
$$
$$
= 10pq + 2q + 4 - 2pq - 4p
$$
$$
= (10pq - 2pq) + 2q + 4 - 4p
$$
$$
= 8pq + 2q + 4 - 4p
$$

So, your expected payoff is:
$$
E_1(p, q) = 8pq + 2q + 4 - 4p
$$

Now, the other player wants to maximize their own expected payoff.

Their payoff:

| P1: A0 | P2: A0 → 10 | P2: A1 → 20 |
|--------|-------------|-------------|
| P1: A1 | P2: A0 → 2 | P2: A1 → 4 |

So their expected payoff:

$$
E_2 = p \cdot [q \cdot 10 + (1 - q) \cdot 20] + (1 - p) \cdot [q \cdot 2 + (1 - q) \cdot 4]
$$

Simplify:

$$
E_2 = p (10q + 20(1 - q)) + (1 - p)(2q + 4(1 - q))
$$
$$
= p (10q + 20 - 20q) + (1 - p)(2q + 4 - 4q)
$$
$$
= p (20 - 10q) + (1 - p)(4 - 2q)
$$
$$
= 20p - 10pq + (4 - 2q) - (4 - 2q)p
$$
$$
= 20p - 10pq + 4 - 2q - 4p + 2qp
$$
$$
= (20p - 4p) + (-10pq + 2pq) + 4 - 2q
$$
$$
= 16p - 8pq + 4 - 2q
$$

So:
$$
E_2(p, q) = 16p - 8pq + 4 - 2q
$$

---

### Step 4: Find Nash equilibrium (mixed strategy)

In a mixed strategy Nash equilibrium, each player must be indifferent between their pure strategies given the other player's mixed strategy.

So, **Player 2** will mix between A0 and A1 only if their expected payoff is equal from both actions, given Player 1's $ p $.

Let’s compute Player 2’s expected payoff from A0 and A1, given Player 1’s mixed strategy with probability $ p $:

- If Player 2 plays A0:
  $$
  EU_2(A0) = p \cdot 10 + (1 - p) \cdot 2 = 10p + 2 - 2p = 8p + 2
  $$

- If Player 2 plays A1:
  $$
  EU_2(A1) = p \cdot 20 + (1 - p) \cdot 4 = 20p + 4 - 4p = 16p + 4
  $$

In equilibrium, for Player 2 to be willing to mix, both must be equal:
$$
8p + 2 = 16p + 4
$$
$$
2 - 4 = 16p - 8p
$$
$$
-2 = 8p \Rightarrow p = -0.25
$$

Wait — this is **impossible**. $ p $ cannot be negative.

So, the equation has no solution in $ [0,1] $. That means Player 2 will **never** be indifferent. Instead, we check which action is better for Player 2 for each $ p $.

Compare:
- $ EU_2(A0) = 8p + 2 $
- $ EU_2(A1) = 16p + 4 $

Since $ 16p + 4 > 8p + 2 $ for all $ p \geq 0 $, because:
$$
16p + 4 - (8p + 2) = 8p + 2 > 0 \quad \text{for all } p \geq 0
$$

So **Player 2 always prefers A1**, no matter what $ p $ is.

Therefore, in equilibrium, **Player 2 will always play A1** (pure strategy).

This is critical.

So, $ q = 0 $, $ 1 - q = 1 $ → Player 2 plays A1 with 100% probability.

---

### Step 5: Now determine your optimal strategy given that Player 2 plays A1

So, if Player 2 plays A1 with probability 1, then:

- If you play A0: you get 0 (from (A0, A1) outcome)
- If you play A1: you get 4 (from (A1, A1) outcome)

So, clearly, you should play **A1** to get 4 points instead of 0.

Therefore, your best response is to play A1 with 100% probability.

So $ p = 0 $ for A0, $ 1 - p = 100 $ for A1.

Wait: no — if you play A0, you get 0; if you play A1, you get 4 → best to play A1.

But is this sustainable? Earlier we found that Player 2 will always prefer A1. So yes — if Player 2 always plays A1, then you best respond by playing A1.

So the equilibrium is:
- Player 1: A1 with probability 1
- Player 2: A1 with probability 1

But wait — let's check if Player 2 would still prefer A1 when you play A1 with probability 1.

If Player 1 plays A1 with probability 1, then:
- Player 2's payoff from A0: $ 1 \cdot 2 + 0 \cdot 10 = 2 $
- Player 2's payoff from A1: $ 1 \cdot 4 + 0 \cdot 20 = 4 $

Still, A1 gives more (4 > 2), so Player 2 still prefers A1.

Yes — so A1 for both is a **pure strategy Nash equilibrium**.

But is there another equilibrium?

Let’s check if Player 1 might mix.

But we can see: if Player 2 plays A1 (which they always do, as it's dominant), then Player 1 should play A1.

So no incentive to mix.

But let's check if there's a mixed equilibrium where Player 2 mixes — we already saw that Player 2 never wants to mix, because A1 gives strictly higher payoff than A0 for all $ p $.

So Player 2's best response is **always A1**.

Therefore, the only equilibrium is:
- Player 1: plays A1
- Player 2: plays A1

So in this game, **your best move** is to play A1 with 100% probability.

But wait — let's double-check what happens if you play A0.

If you play A0 and Player 2 plays A1: you get 0.

If you play A1 and Player 2 plays A1: you get 4.

So clearly better to play A1.

Even if Player 2 might deviate, but in rational play, they won’t — because A1 is always better for them.

So there is no reason to play A0.

---

### Final Answer

You should play A1 with 100% probability.

{"A0": 0, "A1": 100}

===== Response [ID: 1bff9894] [2026-01-24 03:06:30] =====
agent: qwen/qwen3-30b-a3b-instruct-2507(CoT)#P2
response:
Let's analyze this decision-making game step by step.

---

### Step 1: Understand the game structure

We are Player 2 (the second player in the payoff table). The other player is Player 1.

We each choose a strategy: a probability distribution over actions A0 and A1, simultaneously.

The payoffs are defined as follows (Player 2's payoff in each case):

| Player 1 \ Player 2 | A0       | A1       |
|---------------------|----------|----------|
| **A0**              | 10       | 2        |
| **A1**              | 20       | 4        |

Wait — careful: in the description:

- If **you** choose A0 and the other player chooses A0 → you get 10  
- If **you** choose A1 and the other player chooses A0 → you get 20  
- If **you** choose A0 and the other player chooses A1 → you get 2  
- If **you** choose A1 and the other player chooses A1 → you get 4  

So this is **Player 2's** perspective.

Thus, the payoff matrix from **Player 2's** point of view is:

| P1 \ P2 | A0 (P2) | A1 (P2) |
|--------|--------|--------|
| A0 (P1) | 10     | 2      |
| A1 (P1) | 20     | 4      |

So:
- P2 gets 10 if both choose A0
- P2 gets 2 if P1 chooses A0, P2 chooses A1
- P2 gets 2 if P1 chooses A1, P2 chooses A0
- P2 gets 4 if both choose A1

This is a **simultaneous-move game**.

---

### Step 2: Look for dominant strategies

Check if Player 2 has a dominant strategy.

- If Player 1 plays A0:
  - P2 gets 10 (if choose A0)
  - P2 gets 2 (if choose A1)
  → So P2 prefers A0

- If Player 1 plays A1:
  - P2 gets 2 (if choose A0)
  - P2 gets 4 (if choose A1)
  → So P2 prefers A1

So no dominant strategy for Player 2 — depends on what Player 1 does.

Similarly, check for Player 1's best responses:
- If P2 plays A0:
  - P1 gets 10 (if A0), 20 (if A1) → prefers A1
- If P2 plays A1:
  - P1 gets 0 (if A0), 4 (if A1) → prefers A1

So P1 always prefers A1.

→ Thus, **Player 1 has a dominant strategy: A1**

This is key.

---

### Step 3: Rational expectations and equilibrium

Since Player 1 has a dominant strategy (A1), we can **expect** that Player 1 will play A1.

Therefore, as Player 2, we should play **best response to A1**.

If Player 1 plays A1:
- P2 chooses A0 → gets 2
- P2 chooses A1 → gets 4

So best response is **A1** (4 > 2)

Thus, **Player 2’s best response to Player 1’s dominant strategy A1 is to play A1**.

So in equilibrium, both players play A1 → P2 gets 4.

But wait — is that the only equilibrium?

Let’s check if there’s a mixed strategy equilibrium.

---

### Step 4: Mixed strategy Nash Equilibrium (MNE)

Let’s suppose:
- Player 1 plays A0 with probability **p**, A1 with probability **1-p**
- Player 2 plays A0 with probability **q**, A1 with probability **1-q**

We already saw that Player 1 prefers A1 regardless — so **p = 0** in equilibrium.

But let's verify using indifference.

Player 2 wants to choose **q** to make Player 1 indifferent between A0 and A1.

But wait — since Player 1 is the one who can be forced to be indifferent, but **Player 1 has a dominant strategy**, so the only equilibrium is where Player 1 plays A1 with probability 1.

Let’s confirm:

Player 1's expected payoff:
- A0: q*10 + (1-q)*0 = 10q
- A1: q*20 + (1-q)*4 = 20q + 4(1-q) = 20q + 4 - 4q = 16q + 4

Set equal for indifference:
10q = 16q + 4 → -6q = 4 → q = -2/3 → impossible

So no q such that Player 1 is indifferent.

Now compare: 16q + 4 vs 10q

16q + 4 - 10q = 6q + 4 > 0 for all q ≥ 0

So **Player 1 always prefers A1**, regardless of q.

Thus, in rational play, **Player 1 will play A1 with probability 1**.

Therefore, Player 2 knows that Player 1 will play A1.

So Player 2's choice:
- A0 → gets 2
- A1 → gets 4

So Player 2 should choose A1 with probability 1.

---

### Step 5: Final decision

Since Player 1 has a dominant strategy to play A1, and Player 2 can predict that, then best response for Player 2 is to play A1.

Thus, Player 2 should choose:
- A0: 0%
- A1: 100%

This maximizes Player 2's expected payoff at 4, given the certainty that Player 1 plays A1.

Even if we consider possibility of deviation, since Player 1 has no incentive to deviate from A1, the equilibrium is well-supported.

---

### Final Answer

```json
{"A0": 0, "A1": 100}
```

===== Response [ID: ea391efa] [2026-01-24 03:06:42] =====
agent: anthropic/claude-sonnet-4.5(CoT)#P2
response:
Looking at this game step by step:

## Step 1: Understand my payoffs as Player 2

Let me create a payoff matrix for my outcomes based on both players' actions:

**My Payoffs (Player 2):**
```
                    Player 1 chooses A0    Player 1 chooses A1
I choose A0                10                      2
I choose A1                20                      4
```

## Step 2: Analyze which action is better for me

Let me compare my payoffs for each possible action by Player 1:

- **If Player 1 chooses A0:** 
  - I get 10 if I choose A0
  - I get 20 if I choose A1
  - **A1 is better (20 > 10)**

- **If Player 1 chooses A1:**
  - I get 2 if I choose A0  
  - I get 4 if I choose A1
  - **A1 is better (4 > 2)**

## Step 3: Identify dominant strategy

**A1 strictly dominates A0** for me. Regardless of what Player 1 does, I always get more points by choosing A1.

## Step 4: Determine optimal probability distribution

Since A1 is strictly dominant:
- There is no benefit to randomizing
- Playing A1 with 100% probability maximizes my expected payoff regardless of Player 1's strategy
- Any probability assigned to A0 would only decrease my expected payoff

## Conclusion

The optimal strategy is to play A1 with certainty.

===== Response [ID: 32384b16] [2026-01-24 03:06:48] =====
agent: google/gemini-3-flash-preview(CoT)#P2
response:
To maximize the total points received over multiple rounds, it is essential to identify the most beneficial outcome for both players and consider how to achieve it in a simultaneous-move game.

**1. Analysis of the Payoff Matrix:**
As Player 2 (P2), we analyze the options for both myself and Player 1 (P1):
- (P1: A0, P2: A0) -> P1 gets 10, P2 gets 10
- (P1: A0, P2: A1) -> P1 gets 0, P2 gets 20
- (P1: A1, P2: A0) -> P1 gets 6, P2 gets 2
- (P1: A1, P2: A1) -> P1 gets 4, P2 gets 4

**2. Strategy for Player 2 (Me):**
For any choice Player 1 makes, choosing A1 gives me a higher reward (20 > 10 if P1 chooses A0, and 4 > 2 if P1 chooses A1). This makes A1 a strictly dominant strategy for me in a single-round scenario.

**3. Strategy for Player 1:**
- If P1 believes I will play A0, P1's best response is A0 (10 vs 6).
- If P1 believes I will play A1, P1's best response is A1 (4 vs 0).

**4. Long-term Considerations (Multi-round Game):**
The Nash Equilibrium for a single round is (A1, A1), resulting in 4 points each. However, the cooperative outcome (A0, A0) provides significantly more points (10 each). 
In a multi-round game, playing A1 might yield 20 points in the first round (if P1 plays A0), but it would likely cause P1 to switch to A1 for all subsequent rounds to avoid getting 0 points, trapping both players in the (4, 4) outcome. Conversely, playing A0 signals a desire for cooperation. Given that P1’s best response to A0 is A0, and that (10, 10) is much better than (4, 4), the most rational long-term strategy is to establish and maintain the (A0, A0) equilibrium.

**5. Implementation:**
To signal the intent to cooperate and secure 10 points per round rather than 4, I will choose A0 with 100% probability.

```json
{"A0": 100, "A1": 0}
```
